<p><b>Prehistory</b>, also known as <b>pre-literary history</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is the period of <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">human history</a> between the use of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone_tools" title="Stone tools">stone tools</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominin" title="Hominin">hominins</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3.3 million years ago and the beginning of <a href="/wiki/Recorded_history" title="Recorded history">recorded history</a> with the invention of <a href="/wiki/Writing_system" title="Writing system">writing systems</a>. The use of symbols, marks, and images appears very early among humans, but the earliest known writing systems appeared <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 5000 years ago. It took thousands of years for writing systems to be widely adopted, with writing spreading to almost all cultures by the 19th century. The end of prehistory therefore came at very different times in different places, and the term is less often used in discussing societies where prehistory ended relatively recently.
</p><p>In the early <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_valley_civilization" title="Indus valley civilization">Indus valley civilization</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> were the first civilizations to develop their own scripts and to keep historical records, with their neighbors following. Most other civilizations reached the end of prehistory during the following <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">three-age division</a> of prehistory into <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> remains in use for much of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, but is not generally used in those parts of the world where <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">the working of hard metals</a> arrived abruptly from contact with <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_(mixed_ancestry)" title="Eurasian (mixed ancestry)">Eurasian cultures</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>, much of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, and parts of the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>. With some exceptions in <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian civilizations</a> in the Americas, these areas did not develop complex writing systems before the arrival of Eurasians, so their prehistory reaches into relatively recent periods; for example, 1788 is usually taken as the end of the <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Australia" title="Prehistory of Australia">prehistory of Australia</a>.
</p><p>The period when a culture is written about by others, but has not developed its own writing system is often known as the <a href="/wiki/Protohistory" title="Protohistory">protohistory</a> of the culture. By definition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> there are no written records from human prehistory, which we can only know from material <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological</a> evidence: prehistoric materials and human remains. These were at first understood by the collection of <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a> and by analogy with pre-literate societies observed in modern times. The key step to understanding prehistoric evidence is dating, and reliable dating techniques have developed steadily since the nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Further evidence has come from the reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">ancient spoken languages</a>. More recent techniques include forensic chemical analysis to reveal the use and provenance of materials, and genetic analysis of bones to determine kinship and physical characteristics of prehistoric peoples.
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>
<ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe,_Urfa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe%2C_Urfa.jpg/240px-G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe%2C_Urfa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe%2C_Urfa.jpg/360px-G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe%2C_Urfa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe%2C_Urfa.jpg/480px-G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe%2C_Urfa.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe,_Urfa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Massive stone pillars at <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a>, in southeast Turkey, erected for ritual use by early <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> people 11,000 years ago</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caveman_6.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="781" data-file-width="572" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Caveman_6.jpg/170px-Caveman_6.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Caveman_6.jpg/255px-Caveman_6.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Caveman_6.jpg/340px-Caveman_6.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caveman_6.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early sketch imagining an adult and a juvenile from prehistoric times making a stone tool</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_man.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1508" data-file-width="2220" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Prehistoric_man.jpg/240px-Prehistoric_man.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Prehistoric_man.jpg/360px-Prehistoric_man.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Prehistoric_man.jpg/480px-Prehistoric_man.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prehistoric_man.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A nineteenth century concept of early humans in a wilderness</div></div></div>
<p><b>Beginning</b>
</p><p>The term "prehistory" can refer to the vast span of time since the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">beginning</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">Universe</a> or the Earth, but more often it refers to the period since <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> appeared on Earth, or even more specifically to the time since human-like beings appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fagan07_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-fagan07-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renfrew_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-renfrew-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>End</b>
</p><p>The date marking the end of prehistory is typically defined as the advent of the contemporary <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_first_written_accounts" title="List of languages by first written accounts">written historical</a> record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The date consequently varies widely from region to region depending on the date when relevant records become a useful academic resource.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> it is generally accepted that prehistory ended around 3100 BCE, whereas in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a> the end of the prehistoric era is set much more recently, in the 1870s, when the Russian anthropologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicholai_Miklukho-Maklai" title="Nicholai Miklukho-Maklai">Nicholai Miklukho-Maklai</a> spent several years living among native peoples, and described their way of life in a comprehensive treatise.  In Europe the relatively well-documented classical cultures of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Rome</a> had neighbouring cultures, including the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celts</a> and to a lesser extent the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_civilization" title="Etruscan civilization">Etruscans</a>, with little or no writing, and historians must decide how much weight to give to the often highly prejudiced accounts of these protohistoric cultures in Greek and Roman literature.
</p><p><b>Time periods</b>
</p><p>In dividing up human prehistory in Eurasia, historians typically use the <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">three-age system</a>, whereas scholars of pre-human time periods typically use the <a href="/wiki/Chronostratigraphy" title="Chronostratigraphy">well-defined</a> <a href="/wiki/Geologic_record" title="Geologic record">geologic record</a> and its internationally defined <a href="/wiki/Stratum" title="Stratum">stratum</a> base within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Era_(geology)" title="Era (geology)">geologic time scale</a>. The three-age system is the <a href="/wiki/Periodization" title="Periodization">periodization</a> of human prehistory into three consecutive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Time_period" title="Time period">time periods</a>, named for their predominant tool-making technologies:
</p>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minds_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Minds-9">[9]</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<p>For the prehistory of the Americas see <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">Pre-Columbian era</a>. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_term">History of the term</span></h3>
<p>The notion of "prehistory" emerged during the Enlightenment in the work of antiquarians who used the word "primitive" to describe societies that existed before written records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The word "prehistory" first appeared in English in 1836 in the <i>Foreign <a href="/wiki/Quarterly_Review" title="Quarterly Review">Quarterly Review</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The geologic time scale for pre-human time periods, and the <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">three-age system</a> for human prehistory, were systematized during the late nineteenth century in the work of British, German, and Scandinavian <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archeologists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antiquarian" title="Antiquarian">antiquarians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Minds_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Minds-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Means_of_research">Means of research</span></h2>
<p>The main source of information for prehistory is <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> (a branch of anthropology), but some scholars are beginning to make more use of evidence from the natural and social sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iberia_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iberia-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edge_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edge-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pal_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pal-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The primary researchers into human prehistory are archaeologists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_anthropology" title="Physical anthropology">physical anthropologists</a> who use excavation, geologic and geographic surveys, and other scientific analysis to reveal and interpret the nature and behavior of pre-literate and non-literate peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fagan07_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-fagan07-4">[4]</a></sup> Human population <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geneticists" title="Geneticists">geneticists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">historical linguists</a> are also providing valuable insight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renfrew_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-renfrew-5">[5]</a></sup> Cultural anthropologists help provide context for societal interactions, by which objects of human origin pass among people, allowing an analysis of any article that arises in a human prehistoric context.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renfrew_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-renfrew-5">[5]</a></sup> Therefore, data about prehistory is provided by a wide variety of natural and social sciences, such as <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeoastronomy" title="Archaeoastronomy">archaeoastronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Comparative_linguistics" title="Comparative linguistics">comparative linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palynology" title="Palynology">palynology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_anthropology" title="Physical anthropology">physical anthropology</a>, and many others.
</p><p>Human prehistory differs from history not only in terms of its <a href="/wiki/Chronology" title="Chronology">chronology</a>, but in the way it deals with the activities of <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_culture" title="Archaeological culture">archaeological cultures</a> rather than named <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a> or <a href="/wiki/Individual" title="Individual">individuals</a>. Restricted to material processes, remains, and artifacts rather than written records, prehistory is anonymous. Because of this, reference terms that prehistorians use, such as "<a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>", are modern labels with definitions sometimes subject to debate.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Stone_Age">Stone Age</span></h2>
<p>The concept of a "Stone Age" is found useful in the archaeology of most of the world, although in the <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_the_Americas" title="Archaeology of the Americas">archaeology of the Americas</a> it is called by different names and begins with a <a href="/wiki/Lithic_stage" title="Lithic stage">Lithic stage</a>, or sometimes <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indian</a>. The sub-divisions described below are used for Eurasia, and not consistently across the whole area.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Palaeolithic">Palaeolithic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-human-migrations.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="635" data-file-width="889" decoding="async" height="286" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Map-of-human-migrations.svg/400px-Map-of-human-migrations.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Map-of-human-migrations.svg/600px-Map-of-human-migrations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Map-of-human-migrations.svg/800px-Map-of-human-migrations.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map-of-human-migrations.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proposed map of early human migrations, according to <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial</a> <a href="/wiki/Population_genetics" title="Population genetics">population genetics</a> with numbers that are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millennia" title="Millennia">millennia</a> before the present (its accuracy is disputed)</div></div></div>
<p>"Palaeolithic" means "Old Stone Age", and begins with the first use of <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">stone tools</a>. The Paleolithic is the earliest period of the <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>. It extends from the earliest known use of stone tools by <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">hominins</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 3.3 million years ago, to the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr> 11,650 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a> (before the present period).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thoth&amp;Schick_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thoth&amp;Schick-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>The early part of the Palaeolithic is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_Palaeolithic" title="Lower Palaeolithic">Lower Palaeolithic</a>, beginning with the earliest stone tools dated to around 3.3 million years ago at the <a href="/wiki/Lomekwi" title="Lomekwi">Lomekwi</a> site in Kenya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmand_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harmand-16">[16]</a></sup> These tools predate the genus <i>Homo</i> and were probably used by <i><a href="/wiki/Kenyanthropus" title="Kenyanthropus">Kenyanthropus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Control_of_fire" title="Control of fire">control of fire</a> by early hominins during the Lower Palaeolithic Era is uncertain and has at best limited scholarly support. The most widely accepted claim is that <i>H. erectus</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">H. ergaster</a></i> made fires between 790,000 and 690,000 BP in a site at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bnot_Ya%27akov_Bridge" title="Bnot Ya'akov Bridge">Bnot Ya'akov Bridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>. The use of fire enabled early humans to cook food, provide warmth, and have a light source at night.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_Homo_sapiens" title="Early Homo sapiens">Early <i>Homo sapiens</i></a> originated some 200,000 years ago, ushering in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Palaeolithic" title="Middle Palaeolithic">Middle Palaeolithic</a>. Anatomic changes indicating modern language capacity also arise during the Middle Palaeolithic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> During the Middle Palaeolithic Era, there is the first definitive evidence of human use of fire. Sites in Zambia have charred bone and wood that have been dated to 61,000 BP. The systematic <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic_religion" title="Paleolithic religion">burial of the dead</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_music" title="Origin of music">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_art" title="Prehistoric art">early art</a>, and the use of increasingly sophisticated multi-part tools are highlights of the Middle Paleolithic.
</p><p>Throughout the Palaeolithic, humans generally lived as <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer#Social_and_economic_structure" title="Hunter-gatherer">Hunter-gatherer societies</a> tended to be very small and egalitarian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> although hunter-gatherer societies with abundant resources or advanced food-storage techniques sometimes developed sedentary lifestyles with complex social structures such as chiefdoms,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">social stratification</a>. Long-distance contacts may have been established, as in the case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian" title="Indigenous Australian">Indigenous Australian</a> "highways" known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Songlines" title="Songlines">songlines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2983" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG/220px-Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG/330px-Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG/440px-Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dugout_boats_Kierikki_Centre_Oulu_20130526.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dugout_canoe" title="Dugout canoe">Dugout canoe</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Mesolithic, or Middle Stone Age (from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>mesos</i>, 'middle', and <i>lithos</i>, 'stone'), was a period in the development of human <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> between the Palaeolithic and <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a>.
</p><p>The Mesolithic period began with the retreat of glaciers at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> epoch, some 10,000 BP, and ended with <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">the introduction of agriculture</a>, the date of which varied by geographic region. In some areas, such as the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a>, agriculture was already underway by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, and there the Mesolithic is short and poorly defined. In areas with limited <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial" title="Glacial">glacial</a> impact, the term "<a href="/wiki/Epipalaeolithic" title="Epipalaeolithic">Epipalaeolithic</a>" is sometimes preferred.
</p><p>Regions that experienced greater environmental effects as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last ice age</a> ended have a much more evident Mesolithic era, lasting millennia. In <a href="/wiki/Northern_Europe" title="Northern Europe">Northern Europe</a>, societies were able to live well on rich food supplies from the <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshlands</a> fostered by the warmer climate. Such conditions produced distinctive human behaviours that are preserved in the material record, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maglemosian" title="Maglemosian">Maglemosian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azilian" title="Azilian">Azilian</a> cultures. These conditions also delayed the coming of the Neolithic until as late as 4000 BCE (6,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>) in northern Europe.
</p><p>Remains from this period are few and far between, often limited to <a href="/wiki/Midden" title="Midden">middens</a>. In forested areas, the first signs of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> have been found, although this would only begin in earnest during the Neolithic, when more space was needed for <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>.
</p><p>The Mesolithic is characterized in most areas by small composite <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> tools: <a href="/wiki/Microlith" title="Microlith">microliths</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microburin" title="Microburin">microburins</a>. <a href="/wiki/Fishing_tackle" title="Fishing tackle">Fishing tackle</a>, stone <a href="/wiki/Adze" title="Adze">adzes</a>, and wooden objects such as <a href="/wiki/Canoe" title="Canoe">canoes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_(weapon)" title="Bow (weapon)">bows</a> have been found at some sites. These technologies first occur in Africa, associated with the Azilian cultures, before spreading to Europe through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ibero-Maurusian" title="Ibero-Maurusian">Ibero-Maurusian</a> culture of Northern Africa and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kebaran" title="Kebaran">Kebaran</a> culture of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. However, independent discovery is not ruled out.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic">Neolithic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3744" data-file-width="5616" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg/220px-Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg/330px-Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg/440px-Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malta_-_Qrendi_-_Hagar_Qim_and_Mnajdra_Archaeological_Park_-_Hagar_Qim_08_ies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance to the Ġgantija phase temple complex of <a href="/wiki/%C4%A6a%C4%A1ar_Qim" title="Ħaġar Qim">Ħaġar Qim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a>, 3900 BCE<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/220px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/330px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg/440px-N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N%C3%A9olithique_0001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An array of Neolithic artifacts, including bracelets, axe heads, chisels, and polishing tools - Neolithic stone artifacts are by definition polished and, except for specialty items, not chipped</div></div></div>
<p>"Neolithic" means "New Stone Age", from about 10,200 BCE in some parts of the Middle East, but later in other parts of the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellwood_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellwood-23">[23]</a></sup> and ended between 4,500 and 2,000 BCE. Although there were several species of humans during the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, by the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> only <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens sapiens">Homo sapiens sapiens</a></i> remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> This was a period of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technological" title="Technological">technological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social" title="Social">social</a> developments which established most of the basic elements of historical cultures, such as the domestication of crops and <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">animals</a>, and the establishment of permanent settlements and early chiefdoms. The era commenced with the beginning of <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">farming</a>, which produced the "<a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>". It ended when metal tools became widespread (in the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Copper Age</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>; or, in some geographical regions, in the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>). The term <i>Neolithic</i> is commonly used in the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, as its application to cultures in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a> that did not fully develop metal-working technology raises problems.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (October 2019)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Early Neolithic farming was limited to a narrow range of plants, both wild and domesticated, which included <a href="/wiki/Einkorn_wheat" title="Einkorn wheat">einkorn wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Millet" title="Millet">millet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spelt" title="Spelt">spelt</a>, and the keeping of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Origin_of_the_domestic_dog" title="Origin of the domestic dog">dogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Goat#History" title="Goat">goats</a>. By about 6,900–6,400 BCE, it included domesticated <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domesticated_pig" title="Domesticated pig">pigs</a>, the establishment of permanently or seasonally inhabited settlements, and the use of <a href="/wiki/Pottery" title="Pottery">pottery</a>. The Neolithic period saw the development of early <a href="/wiki/Village" title="Village">villages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, animal <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tool" title="Tool">tools</a>, and the onset of the earliest recorded incidents of warfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg/220px-Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg/330px-Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg/440px-Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luni_sul_Mignone_monumental_building.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The monumental building at Luni sul Mignone in <a href="/wiki/Blera" title="Blera">Blera</a>, Italy, 3500 BCE</div></div></div>
<p>Settlements became more permanent, some  with circular houses made of <a href="/wiki/Mudbrick" title="Mudbrick">mudbrick</a> with a single room. Settlements might have a surrounding stone wall to keep domesticated animals in and hostile tribes out. Later settlements have rectangular mud-brick houses where the family lived in single or multiple rooms. Burial findings suggest an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_cult" title="Ancestor cult">ancestor cult</a> with <a href="/wiki/Plastered_human_skulls" title="Plastered human skulls">preserved skulls</a> of the dead. The <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vinča culture">Vinča culture</a> may have created the earliest system of writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Megalith" title="Megalith">megalithic</a> temple complexes of <a href="/wiki/%C4%A0gantija" title="Ġgantija">Ġgantija</a> are notable for their gigantic structures. Although some late Eurasian Neolithic societies formed complex stratified chiefdoms or even states, states evolved in Eurasia only with the rise of metallurgy, and most Neolithic societies on the whole were relatively simple and egalitarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_D._Katz_Rigby_2000_352_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard_D._Katz_Rigby_2000_352-27">[27]</a></sup> Most clothing appears to have been made of animal skins, as indicated by finds of large numbers of bone and antler pins which are ideal for fastening leather. <a href="/wiki/Woolen" title="Woolen">Wool</a> cloth and <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> might have become available during the later Neolithic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> as suggested by finds of perforated stones that (depending on size) may have served as <a href="/wiki/Spindle_(textiles)" title="Spindle (textiles)">spindle whorls</a> or <a href="/wiki/Loom" title="Loom">loom</a> weights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="739" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg/220px-Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg/330px-Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg/440px-Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Los_Millares_recreacion_cuadro.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of a Copper Age walled city, <a href="/wiki/Los_Millares" title="Los Millares">Los Millares</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iberia" title="Iberia">Iberia</a></div></div></div>
<p>In Old World archaeology, the "Chalcolithic", "Eneolithic", or "Copper Age" refers to a transitional period where early <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> metallurgy appeared alongside the widespread use of stone tools. During this period, some weapons and tools were made of copper. This period was still largely Neolithic in character. It is a phase of the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> before it was discovered that adding <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> to <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> formed the harder <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>. The Copper Age was originally defined as a transition between the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> and the Bronze Age. However, because it is characterized by the use of metals, the Copper Age is considered a part of the Bronze Age rather than the Stone Age.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/200px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/300px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/400px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chalcolithic copper mine in <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negev_Desert" title="Negev Desert">Negev Desert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a></div></div></div>
<p>An archaeological site in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> contains the oldest securely dated evidence of copper making at high temperature, from 7,500 years ago. The find in June 2010 extends the known record of copper smelting by about 800 years, and suggests that copper smelting may have been invented independently in separate parts of Asia and Europe at that time, rather than spreading from a single source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-archaeology.ws_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-archaeology.ws-33">[33]</a></sup> The emergence of <a href="/wiki/Metallurgy" title="Metallurgy">metallurgy</a> may have occurred first in the <a href="/wiki/Fertile_Crescent" title="Fertile Crescent">Fertile Crescent</a>, where it gave rise to the Bronze Age in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/4th_millennium_BCE" title="4th millennium BCE">4th millennium BCE</a> (the traditional view), although finds from the <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vinča culture">Vinča culture</a> in Europe have now been securely dated to slightly earlier than those of the Fertile Crescent. <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a> contains evidence of copper mining 9,000 to 7,000 years ago. The process of transition from <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> to Chalcolithic in the Middle East is characterized in archaeological stone tool assemblages by a decline in high quality raw material procurement and use. North Africa and the Nile Valley imported its iron technology from the <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near East</a> and followed the Near Eastern course of Bronze Age and <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> development. However the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and Bronze Age occurred simultaneously in much of Africa.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_into_ancient_history">Transition into ancient history</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protohistory" title="Protohistory">Protohistory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Ancient history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1337" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg/220px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg/330px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg/440px-Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maler_der_Grabkammer_des_Sennudem_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An image of an <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">ox</a>-drawn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plow" title="Plow">plow</a> is accompanied by script, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, c. 1200 BCE</div></div></div>
<p>The Bronze Age is the earliest period in which some civilizations have reached the end of prehistory, by introducing written records. The Bronze Age or parts thereof are thus considered to be part of prehistory only for the regions and civilizations who adopted or developed a system of keeping written records during later periods.  The <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">invention of writing</a> coincides in some areas with the early beginnings of the Bronze Age. Soon after the appearance of writing, people started creating texts including written accounts of events and records of administrative matters.
</p><p>The term Bronze Age refers to a period in human cultural development when the most advanced <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalworking</a> (at least in systematic and widespread use) included techniques for <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> from naturally occurring outcroppings of ores, and then combining them to cast <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>. These naturally occurring ores typically included arsenic as a common impurity. Copper and tin ores are rare, as reflected in the fact that there were no tin bronzes in Western Asia before 3000 BCE. The Bronze Age forms part of the <a href="/wiki/Three-age_system" title="Three-age system">three-age system</a> for prehistoric societies. In this system, it follows the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> in some areas of the world.
</p><p>While copper is a common ore, deposits of tin are rare in the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>, and often had to be traded or carried considerable distances from the few mines, stimulating the creation of extensive trading routes. In many areas as far apart as China and England, the valuable new material was used for weapons but for a long time apparently not available for agricultural tools. Much of it seems to have been hoarded by social elites, and sometimes deposited in extravagant quantities, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ritual_bronze" title="Chinese ritual bronze">Chinese ritual bronzes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_Hoard_Culture" title="Copper Hoard Culture">Indian copper hoards</a> to European <a href="/wiki/Hoard" title="Hoard">hoards</a> of unused axe-heads.
</p><p>By the end of the Bronze Age large states, which are often called empires, had arisen in Egypt, China, <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a>), and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mesopotamia" title="History of Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, all of them literate.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iron_Age">Iron Age</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">Classical antiquity</a></div>
<p>The Iron Age is not part of prehistory for all civilizations who had introduced written records during the Bronze Age. Most remaining civilizations did so during the Iron Age, often through conquest by the empires, which continued to expand during this period. For example, in most of Europe conquest by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> means that the term Iron Age is replaced by "Roman", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallo-Roman" title="Gallo-Roman">Gallo-Roman</a>", and similar terms after the conquest.
</p><p>In archaeology, the Iron Age refers to the advent of <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_metallurgy" title="Ferrous metallurgy">ferrous metallurgy</a>. The adoption of <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> coincided with other changes in some past cultures, often including more sophisticated agricultural practices, religious beliefs and artistic styles, which makes the archaeological Iron Age coincide with the "<a href="/wiki/Axial_Age" title="Axial Age">Axial Age</a>" in the history of philosophy.  Although iron ore is common, the metalworking techniques necessary to use iron are very different from those needed for the metal used earlier, and iron was slow-spreading and for long mainly used for weapons, while bronze remained typical for tools, as well as art.
</p>

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_evolution" title="Timeline of human evolution">Timeline of human evolution</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_prehistory" title="Timeline of human prehistory">Timeline of human prehistory</a></div>
<p>All dates are approximate and conjectural, obtained through research in the fields of <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>. They are all subject to revision due to new discoveries or improved calculations. BP stands for "<a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">Before Present</a> (1950)." BCE stands for Before <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Paleolithic"><a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a></span></h3>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic" title="Lower Paleolithic">Lower Paleolithic</a></dt></dl>
<ul><li>c. 3.3 million BP – Earliest stone tools<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmand_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harmand-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 2.8 million BP – Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> appears</li>
<li>c. 600,000 BP – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunting-gathering" title="Hunting-gathering">Hunting-gathering</a></li>
<li>c. 400,000 BP – <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">Control of fire by early humans</a></li></ul>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a></dt></dl>
<ul><li>c. 300,000–30,000 BP – <a href="/wiki/Mousterian" title="Mousterian">Mousterian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a>) culture in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 200,000 BP – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Anatomically modern humans</a> <i>(<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens sapiens">Homo sapiens sapiens</a>)</i> appear in Africa, one of whose characteristics is a lack of significant body hair compared to other primates. See e.g. <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a>.</li>
<li>c. 170,000–83,000 BP – Invention of <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">clothing</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 75,000 BP – <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba Volcano</a> supereruption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 80,000–50,000 BP – <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> exit Africa as a single population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20160921_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20160921-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-traces_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-traces-38">[38]</a></sup> In the next millennia, descendants from this population migrate to southern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_Malaysia" title="List of islands of Malaysia">the Malay islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, China, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, and the northwestern coast of North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-traces_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-traces-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 80,000–50,000? BP – <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">Behavioral modernity</a>, by this point including <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a> and sophisticated cognition</li></ul>
<dl><dt><a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a></dt></dl>
<ul><li>c. 45,000 BP / 43,000 BCE – Beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2telperronian" title="Châtelperronian">Châtelperronian</a> culture in France.</li>
<li>c. 40,000 BP / 38,000 BCE – First human settlement in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Australia" title="Southern Australia">southern half of the Australian mainland</a>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian" title="Indigenous Australian">indigenous Australians</a> (including the future sites of <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Settlers'_history_rewritten_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Settlers'_history_rewritten-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aboriginal_people_and_place_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aboriginal_people_and_place-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Perth" title="Perth">Perth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Melbourne" title="Melbourne">Melbourne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>)</li>
<li>c. 32,000 BP / 30,000 BCE – Beginnings of <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a> culture, exemplified by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_paintings" title="Cave paintings">cave paintings</a> ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parietal_art" title="Parietal art">parietal art</a>") of <a href="/wiki/Chauvet_Cave" title="Chauvet Cave">Chauvet Cave</a> in France.</li>
<li>c. 30,500 BP / 28,500 BCE – New Guinea is populated by colonists from Asia or Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 30,000 BP / 28,000 BCE – A herd of <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> is slaughtered and butchered by humans in the Vezere Valley in what is today France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 28,000–20,000 BP – <a href="/wiki/Gravettian" title="Gravettian">Gravettian</a> period in Europe. Harpoons, needles, and saws invented.</li>
<li>c. 26,500 BP – <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> (LGM). Subsequently, the ice melts and the glaciers retreat again (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Glacial_Maximum" title="Late Glacial Maximum">Late Glacial Maximum</a>). During this latter period human beings return to Western Europe (see <a href="/wiki/Magdalenian" title="Magdalenian">Magdalenian</a> culture) and enter North America from Eastern Siberia for the first time (see <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Indians" title="Paleo-Indians">Paleo-Indians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-Clovis" title="Pre-Clovis">pre-Clovis</a> culture and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric_migration_and_settlement_of_the_Americas_from_Asia" title="Prehistoric migration and settlement of the Americas from Asia">Settlement of the Americas</a>).</li>
<li>c. 26,000 BP / 24,000 BCE – People around the world use fibers to make baby-carriers, clothes, bags, baskets, and nets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 25,000 BP / 23,000 BCE – A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doln%C3%AD_V%C4%9Bstonice_(archaeology)" title="Dolní Věstonice (archaeology)">settlement</a> consisting of huts built of rocks and <a href="/wiki/Mammoth" title="Mammoth">mammoth</a> bones is founded near what is now <a href="/wiki/Doln%C3%AD_V%C4%9Bstonice" title="Dolní Věstonice">Dolní Věstonice</a> in <a href="/wiki/Moravia" title="Moravia">Moravia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>. This is the oldest human permanent settlement that has been found by archaeologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 23,000 BP / 21,000 BCE – Small-scale trial cultivation of plants in <a href="/wiki/Ohalo_II" title="Ohalo II">Ohalo II</a>, a hunter-gatherers' sedentary camp on the shore of the Sea of Galilee, Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 16,000 BP / 14,000 BCE – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wisent" title="Wisent">Wisent</a> sculpted in clay deep inside the cave now known as Le Tuc d'Audoubert in the French Pyrenees near what is now the border of Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 14,800 BP / 12,800 BCE – The <a href="/wiki/African_humid_period" title="African humid period">Humid Period</a> begins in North Africa. The region that would later become the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> is wet and fertile, and the <a href="/wiki/Aquifer" title="Aquifer">aquifers</a> are full.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span id="Mesolithic.2FEpipaleolithic"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesolithic/Epipaleolithic"><a href="/wiki/Mesolithic" title="Mesolithic">Mesolithic</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epipaleolithic" title="Epipaleolithic">Epipaleolithic</a></span></h3>
<ul><li>c. 12,500 to 9,500 BCE – <a href="/wiki/Natufian_culture" title="Natufian culture">Natufian culture</a>: a culture of sedentary hunter-gatherers who may have cultivated <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">Rye</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> (<a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neolithic_2"><a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a></span></h3>
<ul><li>c. 9,400–9,200 BCE – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Figs" title="Figs">Figs</a> of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parthenocarpic" title="Parthenocarpic">parthenocarpic</a> (and therefore sterile) type are cultivated in the early <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> village <a href="/wiki/Gilgal_I" title="Gilgal I">Gilgal I</a> (in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jordan_Valley_(Middle_East)" title="Jordan Valley (Middle East)">Jordan Valley</a>, 13 km north of <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a>). The find predates the domestication of <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a>, and may thus be the first known instance of agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 9,000 BCE – Circles of T-shaped stone pillars erected at <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6bekli_Tepe" title="Göbekli Tepe">Göbekli Tepe</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Anatolia_Region" title="Southeastern Anatolia Region">Southeastern Anatolia Region</a> of Turkey during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-pottery_Neolithic_A" title="Pre-pottery Neolithic A">pre-pottery Neolithic A</a> (PPNA) period. As yet unexcavated structures at the site are thought to date back to the epipaleolithic.</li>
<li>c. 8,000 BC / 7000 BCE – In northern <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, now northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>, cultivation of barley and wheat begins. At first they are used for <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gruel" title="Gruel">gruel</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soup" title="Soup">soup</a>, eventually for <a href="/wiki/Bread" title="Bread">bread</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> In early agriculture at this time the planting stick is used, but it is replaced by a primitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plow" title="Plow">plow</a> in subsequent centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Around this time, a round stone tower, now preserved at about 8.5 meters high and 8.5 meters in diameter is built in <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chalcolithic_2"><a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a></span></h3>
<ul><li>c. 3,700 BCE – <a href="/wiki/Pictogram" title="Pictogram">Pictographic</a> <a href="/wiki/Proto-writing" title="Proto-writing">proto-writing</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform#History" title="Cuneiform">proto-cuneiform</a>, appears in <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, and records begin to be kept. According to the majority of specialists, the first Mesopotamian writing (actually still pictographic proto-writing at this stage) was a tool for record-keeping that had little connection to the spoken language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>c. 3,300 BCE – Approximate date of death of "<a href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi" title="Ötzi">Ötzi</a> the Iceman", found preserved in ice in the <a href="/wiki/%C3%96tztal_Alps" title="Ötztal Alps">Ötztal Alps</a> in 1991. A copper-bladed axe, which is a characteristic technology of this era, was found with the corpse.</li>
<li>c. 3,000 BCE – <a href="/wiki/Stonehenge" title="Stonehenge">Stonehenge</a> construction begins. In its first version, it consisted of a circular ditch and bank, with 56 wooden posts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></li></ul>
